دکان بازار میں کرایہ پر لی تا کہ تجارت کرے گا مال ضائع ہو گیا یا اسی طرح دکان یا مکان کرایہ پر لیا پھر مفلس ہو گیا اور اس کو دین لازم ہو گئے ان دیون کو اسی کرایہ کے مکان : کے شمن سے ادا کیا جا سکتا ہے قاضی عقد اجارہ کو فسخ کرے اور اسکو دین کے بدلے بیچ دے
وكذا من استاجر دكانا في السوق ليتجر فيه فذهب ماله وكذا اذا آجر دكانا او دارا ثم افلس ولزمته ديون لا يقدر على قضائها الا بثمن ما آجر فسخ القاضى العقد وباعها في الدين لان فى الجرى على موجب العقد
 الزام ضرر زائد لم يستحق بالعقد وهو الحبس لانه قد لا يصدق على عدم مال آخر ثم قوله فسخ القاضى العقد اشارة الى انه يفتقر الى قضاء القاضى فى النقض وهكذا ذكر فى الزيادات في عذر الدين وقال في الجامع الصغير وكل ما ذكرنا نه عذر فان الاجارة فيه تنتقض وهذا يدل على انه لا يحتاج فيه الى قضاء القاضى ووجهه ان هذا بمنزلة العيب قبل القبض فى المبيع على مامر فينفرد العاقد بالفسح ووجه الاول انه فصل مجتهد فيه فلابد من الزام القاضى ومنهم من وفق فقال ان كان العذر ظاهرا لا يحتاج الى القضاء وان كان غير ظاهر كالدين يحتاج الى القضاء لظهور العذر
ترجمہ ایسے ہی اگر کسی نے دکان کرایہ پر لی بازار میں تاکہ تجارت کر لے۔ پھر اس کا مال جا تا رہا۔ اسی طرح جب دکان یا مکان کرایہ پر دیا پھر وہ مفلس ہو گیا اور اس کے ذمہ اتنے قرضہ ہو گئے جن کو ادا نہیں کر سکتا مگر کرایہ پر دی ہوئی چیز کی قیمت سے تو ضیح کر دے قاضی عقد اور بیچ دے اس کو قرض میں کیونکہ مقتضائے عقد پر چلنے میں ضرر زائد لازم کرنا ہے جس کا عقد سے مستحق نہیں ہوا تھا۔ اور وہ قید ہو جانا ہے کیونکہ کبھی دوسرا مال نہ ہونے پر اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جاتی۔ پھر یہ کہنا کہ قاضی عقد فسخ کر دے اشارہ ہے اس بات کی طرف کہ عقد توڑنے میں قضاء قاضی کی ضرورت ہے اور یوں ہی زیادات میں عذر قرضہ کی بابت مذکور ہے۔ اور جامع صغیر میں میدہ کہا ہے کہ وہ تمام امور جن کو ہم نے ذکر کیا کہ وہ عذر ہیں ان میں اجارہ ٹوٹ جائے گا یہ اس پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ توڑنے میں قضاء قاضی کی ضرورت نہیں ۔ اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ یہ مبیع میں قبضہ سے پہلے عیب کے درجہ میں ہے جیسا کہ گذر چکا۔ پس فسخ کرنے میں عاقد مستقل ہوگا اور قول اول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ یہ مجتہد فیہ مسئلہ ہے تو قاضی کا لازم کرنا ضروری ہے اور بعض مشائخ نے تطبیق دی ہے پس کہا ہے کہ اگر عذر ظاہر تو قضاء کی ضرورت نہیں اور اگر غیر ظاہر ہو جیسے قرضہ تو ظہور عذر کے لئے قضاء کی ضرورت ہے۔
تشريح قوله وكذا من استاجر دكانا .... الخ (۳) ایک شخص نے بازار میں ایک دکان کرایہ پر لی تا کہ اس میں تجارت کرے پھر اس کا مال جا تا رہا تو وہ لامحالہ اجارہ فسخ کرے گا یہ تو عذر مستاجر کی مثال ہے۔ عذر موجر کی مثال یہ ہے کہ اس نے اپنی دکان یا مکان کرایہ پر دیا پھر مفلس ہو گیا اور اس پر اتنے قرضے چڑھ گئے جن کی ادائیگی اس کے بغیر ممکن نہیں کہ جو چیز اس نے کرایہ پر دی ہے اس کو فروخت کرے اس کے دیون سے ادا کر کے تو یہ مسیح عذر ہے پس قاضی اجارہ نسخ کر کے اس کا مکان یا دکان قرضہ میں فروخت کرے گا۔ کیونکہ مقتضائے عقد پر چلنے میں اس کے ذمہ ایسا زائد ضر لازم ہو جاتا ہے جس کا وہ عقد سے مستحق نہیں ہوا تھا اور وہ ضر ر زائد یہ ہے کہ قرضہ ادا نہ کرے تو گرفتار کر کے قید خانہ میں ڈال دیا جائے گا بشرطیکہ دوسرا مال ہو اور کبھی دوسرا مال نہ ہونے پر اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جاتی۔
قـولـه ثـم قوله فسخ القاضي ... الخ ۔ یعنی امام قدوری نے مختصر میں جو یہ کہا ہے فسخ القاضی العقد یہ اس بات کی طرف اشارہ ہے کہ عقد توڑنے میں قضاء قاضی کی ضرورت ہے اور عذر قرضہ کی بابت زیادات میں بھی یوں ہی مذکور ہے اور اسی کو شمس الائمہ سرخسی نے صحیح کہا ہے اور جامع صغیر میں ہے کہ جن امور کو ہم نے عذر بیان کیا ہے ان تمام میں اجارہ ٹوٹ جائے گا یہ اس پر دال ہے کہ اجارہ فنح ہونے میں قضاء قاضی کی ضرورت نہیں اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اجارہ میں یہ عذر ایساہے جیسے بیچ میں قبضہ سے پہلے عیب کہ مشتری خود نسخ کر سکتا ہے اور پہلے قول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ یہ مسلہ مجتہد فیہ ہے چنانچہ ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک اجارہ فسخ نہیں ہوتا اس لئے ضروری ہوا کہ قاضی اپنے حکم
His saying: 'And it is like one who hires...' - From here he mentions several forms of excuses that necessitate rescission. 1) A person hired a dentist to extract a painful tooth, then before extraction the pain disappeared; the ijarah is rescinded because maintaining it would require extracting a healthy tooth, which was not obligatory on the hirer under the contract. 2) Someone hired a cook for a walima feast, then the wife took khul' or died; the ijarah is rescinded, because if it were not rescinded and performance required, the hirer would suffer an additional harm not entitled by the contract. He hired a shop in the market to trade, then his capital was lost; similarly, if he leased a shop or house and then became insolvent, incurring debts that can only be paid by selling the leased property, then the qadi rescinds the contract and sells it for the debt. Because continuing according to the contract would cause an additional harm (imposing a detriment) that was not entitled by the contract, namely imprisonment, since he may not be believed regarding having no other wealth. Then his saying 'the qadi rescinds the contract' indicates that the rescission requires the qadi's ruling. This is also mentioned in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt. In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is said: 'All that we have mentioned as an excuse, the ijarah is rescinded in it.' This indicates that the qadi's ruling is not needed. The reason is that this is like a defect before possession in a sale, as previously, so the contracting party acts alone in rescission. The reason for the first view is that it is a matter of ijtihad, so the qadi's imposition is necessary. Some scholars reconciled: if the excuse is apparent, no qadi needed; if not apparent (like debt), the qadi is needed to establish the excuse. Translation: Similarly, if someone hired a shop in the market to trade, then his capital was lost, or if he leased a shop or house then became insolvent with debts he cannot pay except by the price of what he leased, then the qadi rescinds the contract and sells it for the debt, because continuing on the contract would impose an extra harm not entitled by the contract. That extra harm is imprisonment, because he may not be believed regarding having no other wealth. Then his saying that the qadi rescinds the contract indicates that the rescission requires the qadi's ruling. Similarly in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt. In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is said that all the matters we mentioned as excuses, the ijarah is rescinded; this indicates that the qadi's ruling is not needed. The reason is that this is like a defect before possession in a sale, so the contracting party acts alone in rescission. The reason for the first view is that it is a matter of ijtihad, so the qadi's imposition is necessary. Some scholars reconciled: if the excuse is apparent, no qadi needed; if not apparent like debt, the qadi is needed to establish the excuse. Explanation of his saying 'and likewise if he hired a shop...': 3) A person hired a shop in the market to trade, then his capital was lost; he will necessarily rescind the ijarah. This is an example of the hirer's excuse. An example of the lessor's excuse: he leased his shop or house, then became insolvent with debts that cannot be paid except by selling the leased property; this is a valid excuse. So the qadi rescinds the ijarah and sells the house or shop for the debt. Because continuing on the contract would cause an extra harm not entitled by the contract: that extra harm is that if he does not pay the debt, he will be arrested and imprisoned, provided he has no other wealth, and sometimes he is not believed regarding having no other wealth. His saying then: 'the qadi rescinds the contract' means that Imam al-Quduri said in his Mukhtasar: 'the qadi rescinds the contract.' This indicates that the rescission requires the qadi's ruling. The same is mentioned in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi considered it correct. In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is said that all the matters we mentioned as excuses, the ijarah is rescinded; this indicates that rescission does not need the qadi's ruling. The reason is that this excuse in ijarah is like a defect before possession in a sale, where the buyer can rescind himself. The reason for the first view is that this is a matter of ijtihad; since the three Imams hold that ijarah is not rescinded, it is necessary that the qadi passes his ruling.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides practical examples of excuses that justify rescinding an ijarah contract according to the Hanafi school, and then discusses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether rescission requires a qadi's ruling. The examples include: (1) hiring a dentist for an extraction, then the pain stops – extracting a healthy tooth would be an extra hardship not contracted for; (2) hiring a cook for a walima, then the wife divorces or dies – the walima's purpose is lost; (3) hiring a shop for trade, then losing capital – trading becomes impossible; (4) a lessor becoming insolvent and needing to sell the rented property to pay debts – otherwise he would be imprisoned, an extra harm not contracted for. The text then presents two Hanafi opinions: one from al-Ziyadat and al-Quduri that requires a qadi's rescission (due to the matter being ijtihadi and contested by other schools), and another from al-Jami' al-Saghir that allows the affected party to rescind unilaterally (analogizing to a defect before possession in a sale). A reconciliation is offered: if the excuse is apparent (e.g., loss of capital), no qadi needed; if not apparent (e.g., hidden debt), a qadi is required to verify the excuse. The commentary explains that the extra harm in the insolvency case is imprisonment for debt.

Key Terms
قاضی (qadi): A judge who has authority to rescind contracts when needed, especially in contested matters.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departure from qiyas for a stronger reason, as in analogizing ijarah rescission to defect before possession.
جامع صغیر (al-Jami' al-Saghir): A foundational Hanafi work by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing main (zahir al-riwayah) positions.
زیادات (al-Ziyadat): Another foundational Hanafi work by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled view in the Hanafi school, when is a qadi's ruling necessary to rescind an ijarah contract due to an excuse?
A) Never, the party can always rescind
B) Always, regardless of the excuse
C) Only when the excuse is not apparent, like hidden debt
D) Only when the excuse is apparent, like loss of capital

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, rescission for excuses is allowed; if the excuse is apparent, the party acts alone; if non-apparent, the qadi must intervene.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
8 min
Excuse of Debt in Ijarah and Qadi Requirement
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qadi requirement for ijarah termination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on termination of ijarah due to excuse by distinguishing between manifest and hidden excuses and determining whether a qadi's ruling is required.

Core Idea
An excuse in ijarah: manifest excuses need no qadi; hidden ones do.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a practical problem in ijarah contracts: what happens when a lessee loses capital or a lessor becomes insolvent? The Hanafi school has two different transmissions on whether a qadi must terminate the contract. The resolution lies in distinguishing between manifest excuses that are obvious and hidden excuses that require verification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting transmissions
Locate the two positions: one from al-Ziyadat requiring a qadi's ruling for termination, and one from al-Jami' al-Saghir stating that no qadi is needed.
The first view is attributed to al-Ziyadat, the second to al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
For al-Ziyadat: termination is a matter of ijtihad, so the qadi's imposition is needed. For al-Jami' al-Saghir: the excuse is analogous to a defect before possession in sale, where the contractor can annul alone.
The qiyas to sale defect is a key analogy.
3
Apply the reconciliation
Some mashayikh reconciled: if the excuse is manifest (e.g., overt loss of capital), no qadi needed; if hidden (e.g., debt), qadi is needed to verify and impose termination.
Manifest excuses are self-evident; hidden excuses require judicial scrutiny.
4
Analyze why hidden excuses require qadi
The qadi ensures the excuse is genuine and imposes termination, avoiding unilateral rescission that may be contested.
The three Imams held that ijarah is not annulled at all, hence the need for qadi's order.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Shaykh Mahbubi and Qadikhan authenticated the reconciled view, which is the operative position for fatwa.
This reconciled view balances legal certainty and practicality.

ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک اجارہ فسخ نہیں ہوتا اس لئے ضروری ہوا کہ قاضی اپنے حکم سے نسخ اجارہ لازم کرے۔ اور بعض مشائخ نے ان دونوں روایتوں میں یوں تطبیق دی ہے کہ اگر عذر بالکل ظاہر ہو تو حکم قاضی کی ضرورت ہوگی۔ شیخ محبوبی اور قاضی خان نے اس کی تصحیح کی ۔ ہے۔
Book of Ijarat. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 12. Imposition of excessive harm not deserved by the contract, which is imprisonment, because he may not be believed regarding having no other wealth. Then his saying: 'The qadi annuls the contract' is an indication that it requires the qadi's ruling in annulment. Likewise it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'And everything we mentioned as an excuse, the ijarah is annulled therein.' This indicates that it does not require the qadi's ruling. Its face is that this is like a defect before possession in the sale as has passed, so the contractor alone may annul. The face of the first is that it is a matter of ijtihad, so the qadi's imposition is necessary. And some of them reconciled, saying: If the excuse is manifest, it does not need qadi; if it is not manifest, like debt, it needs qadi for the manifestation of the excuse. Translation: Similarly, if someone rented a shop in the market to trade, then his capital was lost. Likewise, when he rented out a shop or house, then he became insolvent and incurred so many debts that he cannot pay them except by selling the rented property, then the qadi annuls the contract and sells it for the debt. Because following the contract would require excessive harm that was not deserved by the contract, and that is imprisonment, because sometimes he is not believed regarding having no other wealth. Then his saying that the qadi annuls the contract is an indication that annulment requires the qadi's ruling, and likewise it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is said that all matters we mentioned as excuses, the ijarah is broken therein. This indicates that annulment does not require the qadi's ruling. The reason is that this is like a defect before possession in the sale, as passed, so the contractor is independent in annulment. The reason for the first view is that this is a matter of ijtihad, so the qadi's imposition is necessary. And some mashayikh reconciled, saying: If the excuse is manifest, no need for qadi; if not manifest, like debt, then for the manifestation of the excuse, qadi is needed. Commentary on his saying 'wa kadha man istajara dukkanan...' (3) A person rented a shop in the market to trade, then his capital was lost, so he will inevitably annul the ijarah. This is an example of the lessee's excuse. The example of the lessor's excuse: He rented out his shop or house, then became insolvent and incurred debts that cannot be paid except by selling the rented property, then it is a valid excuse. So the qadi annuls the ijarah and sells his house or shop for the debt. Because following the contract imposes such excessive harm that he was not entitled to by the contract, and that excessive harm is that if he does not pay the debt, he will be arrested and imprisoned, provided he has no other wealth, and sometimes he is not believed regarding having no other wealth. His saying 'thumma qawluhu faskha al-qadi...' i.e., Imam al-Quduri said in al-Mukhtasar: 'The qadi annuls the contract' indicates that annulment requires the qadi's ruling. Likewise it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat regarding the excuse of debt. Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi considered it correct. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'All matters we mentioned as excuses, the ijarah is annulled therein.' This indicates that annulment does not require the qadi's ruling. The reason is that in ijarah, this excuse is like a defect before possession in sale, where the buyer can annul himself. The reason for the first view is that this is a matter of ijtihad, and indeed the three Imams hold that ijarah is not annulled, so it became necessary that the qadi impose annulment by his order. And some mashayikh reconciled between these two narrations: If the excuse is completely manifest, there is no need for qadi's order; if it is hidden, there is need. Shaykh Mahbubi and Qadikhan authenticated this.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the ruling when a lessee or lessor becomes unable to continue the ijarah due to debt or loss of capital. The core issue is whether the contract can be terminated without a qadi's ruling. Two positions from Hanafi sources are presented: al-Ziyadat requires qadi; al-Jami' al-Saghir does not. The reconciliation by later scholars states that manifest excuses (like loss of capital) do not need qadi, while hidden excuses (like debt) do, because the qadi verifies the hidden condition. The commentary then expands on examples and the reasoning.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A valid excuse (like loss of capital or debt) that allows termination of an ijarah contract to prevent excessive harm.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or termination of a contract, here of an ijarah, due to an excuse.
قضاء القاضى (qada' al-qadi): The ruling or order of a qadi, required for termination when the excuse is hidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled view in this passage, when is a qadi's ruling required to terminate an ijarah contract due to an excuse?
A) For all excuses
B) For manifest excuses only
C) For hidden excuses like debt only
D) Never, the contractor can always terminate

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ijarah termination for excuse: manifest excuses allow self-help; hidden excuses require qadi's order.

Change of Mind as Excuse in Ijarah of Animals
Ruling on change of intention for travel as an excuse for lessee but not lessor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the lessee's and lessor's change of mind in ijarah of animals, apply the excusing conditions, and state the Hanafi views on lessor's illness as an excuse.

Core Idea
Lessee's change of travel plans excuses contract termination; lessor's change does not, and his illness is disputed.

When a contract of hire (ijarah) is made for the use of an animal for travel, circumstances may change. This passage from al-Hidayah explores when such changes constitute a legally valid excuse (udhr) to terminate the contract. The key distinction is between the lessee who would suffer harm if forced to continue, and the lessor who can still fulfill the contract through an agent. An intra-Hanafi disagreement between the original riwaya and Imam al-Karkhi over the lessor's illness adds nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: the lessee changes his mind about travel, and the lessor changes his mind. Determine the ruling for each from the text.
The lessee's change of mind is an excuse; the lessor's change is not.
2
Analyze the rationale for the lessee's excuse
The text provides three examples of harm: missing Hajj time, debtor being present (obviating the need to travel), or becoming poor for trade. This illustrates the principle that contracts are not enforced when they cause excessive harm.
The principle is daf' al-darar (repelling harm).
3
Examine the lessor's position
The lessor's change of mind is not an excuse because he can still fulfill the contract by sending the animal with a student or employee. This shows that the contract remains possible.
The possibility of delegation (tawkil) is key.
4
Investigate the dispute on lessor's illness
The original riwaya (al-Mabsut) states that illness is like change of mind: not an excuse, since delegation is still possible. Al-Karkhi objects, arguing illness inevitably involves harm that cannot be delegated.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (legitimate difference).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the original view, the lessee must pay rent even if the lessor is ill; under al-Karkhi, the lessee may terminate without penalty. This affects the lessee's financial liability.
The mufta bihi is not specified in the passage; later practice may follow the original riwaya.

Source Text

The manifest excuse is like hiring a blacksmith to extract a tooth or a cook to cook a wedding feast. He rented a mount to travel on it, then an obstacle to travel occurred, that is an excuse. And whoever rents an animal to travel on it, then he changes his mind about travel, it is an excuse because if he follows the contract, excessive harm would befall him, because perhaps he was going for Hajj and its time passed, or to seek his debtor and he showed up, or for trade and he became poor. But if the lessor changes his mind, that is not an excuse because he can sit and send the animals with his student or employee. And if the lessor falls sick and sits, the answer is the same according to the original riwaya. Al-Karkhi mentioned that it is an excuse because it is not free from harm, so harm is repelled from him in necessity, not in choice. Translation: Someone rented an animal to travel on it, then his opinion changed, that is an excuse because if he follows the contract, excessive harm would befall him, because possibly he was going for Hajj and its time passed, or to seek his debtor and he showed up, or for trade and he became poor. If the lessor's opinion changed, it is not an excuse because it is possible for him to sit and send the animals with his student or employee. And if the lessor fell sick and sat, the answer is the same according to the original riwaya of al-Mabsut. Imam al-Karkhi mentioned that it is also an excuse because it is not free from harm, so this harm is repelled from him in necessity, not in choice. Commentary on his saying 'thumma badalahu...': In Jamhara and Jamal etc., 'bada' (with nunation) means to become manifest, and 'badalahu fi hadha al-amr' is said when the first opinion changes. From this they say 'fulan dhu badawat' meaning a person of varying opinions, i.e., no firmness in his opinion. 'Thumma badalahu min al-safar' means: it appeared to him that the opposite of what he intended from travel is better. So 'bada' is not a verb with a hidden subject; rather, by the context of speech, the subject is implied, so the pronoun referring to it is correct, as in their saying 'in kana ghadan fa'tini', meaning 'if what we are in is tomorrow, come to me'. The meaning of the book's phrase and the ruling of the issue are clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses various excuses in ijarah. It first gives examples of manifest excuses: hiring a blacksmith to extract a tooth (the tooth might be extracted) or a cook to cook a wedding feast (the feast might be cancelled). Then it addresses the case of renting an animal for travel: if the lessee changes his mind, it is an excuse because following the contract would cause harm (e.g., missing Hajj, losing a business opportunity). However, if the lessor changes his mind, it is not an excuse because he can still send the animal with someone else. There is a dispute regarding the lessor's illness: the original riwaya says it is not an excuse; Imam al-Karkhi says it is, because it involves harm. The commentary then explains the linguistic point of 'bada' (to appear/change).

Key Terms
بدا له (bada lahu): A change of mind; here specifically the lessee's change of intention regarding travel, which is considered a valid excuse.
مكارى (mukari): The lessor who rents out an animal or vehicle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, it is a valid excuse

D) Only if the lessor cannot find someone to send the animal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the lessee's change of intention excuses termination due to harm, while the lessor's change does not; illness of the lessor is a point of intra-madhhab disagreement.

Selling the Rented Slave and Tailor's Bankruptcy as Excuses in Ijarah
Rulings on two excuses: selling rented slave (not excuse) and tailor's bankruptcy (excuse)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid excuses in Hanafi ijarah by applying the criterion of dharar (harm) vs loss of profit (istirbah).

Core Idea
Losing potential profit is not harm; losing one's capital is.

This passage from the Hanafi corpus presents two contrasting rulings on excuses (udhr) in ijarah. Understanding the difference between dharar and mere loss of profit is key to applying the Hanafi position. The text explains that selling the rented slave only deprives the lessor of potential profit, which is not a recognized harm, while a tailor's bankruptcy destroys his capital, making continuation harmful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the two cases: (1) lessor sells the rented slave, (2) tailor (lessee) becomes bankrupt. Note the ruling for each.
2
Extract the ruling and reasoning
The first case is ruled 'not an excuse' because the lessor only loses potential profit (istirbah). The second is 'an excuse' because the tailor loses his capital (rās māl), causing dharar.
The reasoning (ta'lil) is key to understanding the distinction.
3
Define key terms: dharar vs istirbah
Dharar is actual harm that makes contract continuation oppressive. Istirbah is mere loss of potential profit, not considered harm.
This distinction is a consistent Hanafi principle in ijarah and other contracts.
4
Apply the distinction to other cases
Practice by determining whether other events (e.g., lessor's bankruptcy) constitute excuses using the same criterion.
Focus on whether the event affects the party's capital (rās māl) or only potential gain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے اپنا غلام کرایہ پر دیا پھر اسے بیچ دیا یہ عذر نہیں ہے ومن آجر عبده ثم باعه فليس بعذر لانه لا يلزمه الضرر بالمضى على موجب العقد وانما يفوته الاسترباح وانه امر زائد قال و اذا استاجر الخياط غلاما فافلس وترك العمل فهو عذر لانه يلزم الضرر بالمضى على موجب العقد لفوات مقصوده وهو رأس ماله وتأويل المسألة خياط يعمل لنفسه اما الذي يخيط باجر فرأس ماله الخيط والمخيط والمقراض فلا يتحقق الافلاس فيه وان اراد ترك الخياطة وان يعمل في الصرف فهو
Someone rented out his slave, then sold him, that is not an excuse. And whoever rents out his slave then sells him, it is not an excuse because he does not suffer harm by following the contract; he only loses potential profit, which is an additional matter. He said: And if a tailor rents a slave, then becomes bankrupt and stops work, that is an excuse because he suffers harm by following the contract due to loss of his objective, which is his capital. The interpretation of the issue: a tailor who works for himself; as for one who sews for hire, his capital is thread, needle, and scissors – bankruptcy does not apply to him. And if he intends to quit tailoring and work in money exchange, then he...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings on excuses in ijarah. First: if the lessor sells the rented slave, that is not a valid excuse because the lessor does not suffer harm from continuing the contract; he only loses potential profit, which is not considered harm. Second: if a tailor (who works for himself) rents a slave and then becomes bankrupt and stops work, that is a valid excuse because his capital is lost and continuing the contract would cause harm. The passage distinguishes between a tailor who works for himself (his capital is his labor) and one who sews for hire (his capital is thread, needle, etc., so bankruptcy is not applicable). The text cuts off while discussing the case of the tailor intending to change professions.

Key Terms
استرباح (istirbah): Gaining profit; here it refers to the lessor's potential profit from selling the slave, which is not a sufficient harm to terminate the ijarah.
إفلاس (iflas): Bankruptcy; here applied to a tailor who rents a slave and then becomes bankrupt, which is a valid excuse because it affects his capital (his labor).
عذر (udhr): Excuse; a legally recognized ground for terminating a contract or avoiding liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because selling is allowed in ijarah
B) Because the lessor does not suffer harm, only loses potential profit
C) Because the slave is not a necessary part of the contract
D) Because the lessee can still use the slave

Key Takeaway
Selling the subject of ijarah is not an excuse because it only causes loss of profit, not harm; bankruptcy is an excuse if it destroys the lessee's capital, causing actual harm.

When the Hirer Changes His Mind: A Valid Excuse in Ijarah
Ruling with rational proof on udhr in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the hirer's change of intention in ijarah and explain why it constitutes a valid excuse (udhr) based on the Hanafi principle of preventing harm.

Core Idea
A hirer's change of travel plans is an excuse to end ijarah because proceeding causes excessive harm; the owner's change is not, as he avoids harm.

This passage addresses a common scenario in ijarah of transport: what happens if the hirer decides not to travel after hiring the animal? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the hirer and the owner, based on who suffers harm. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their positions
Note that the text gives different rulings for when the hirer changes his mind (it is an excuse) versus when the owner/mukari changes his mind (it is not an excuse).
2
Understand the harm analysis
The hirer would suffer excessive harm (e.g., missing Hajj time, debtor appearing, bankruptcy) if forced to proceed. The owner suffers no harm because he can still earn by sending the animal with a representative.
The harm to the hirer is unavoidable, so the contract can be terminated to prevent harm.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
The ruling is based on the general principle that harm must be prevented (la darar wa la dirar). The hirer's harm is unavoidable, so udhr is granted; the owner's harm is avoidable, so no udhr.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) using the concept of darar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن استاجر دابة ليسافر عليه ثم بدا له من السفر فهو عذر لانه لو مضى على موجب العقد يلزمه ضرر زائد لانه ربما يذهب للحج فذهب وقته او لطلب غريمه فحضر او للتجارة فافتقر وان بدا للمكارى فلیس دالك بعذر لانه يمكنه ان يقعد ويبعث الدواب على يد تلميذه او اجيره ولو مرض المواجر فقعد فكذا الجواب على رواية الاصل وذكر الكرخى انه عذر لانه لا يعرى عن ضرر فيدفع عنه عند الضرورة دون الاختيار
ترجمہ کسی نے جانور کرایہ پرلیا تا کہ اس پر سفر کرے پھر اس کی رائے بدل گئی تو یہ عذر ہے کیونکہ اگر وہ موجب عقد پر چلے تو اس کوضرر زائد لازم ہوگا اس لئے کہ ہو سکتا ہے وہ حج کو جاتا ہو اس کا وقت نکل گیا یا قرضدار کی تلاش میں جاتا ہومگر وہ حاضر ہو گیا یا برائے تجارت جانا چاہتا ہومگر وہ مفلس ہو گیا اور اگر کرایہ پر دینے والے کی رائے بدلی تو یہ عذر نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کو یہ ممکن ہے کہ خود بیٹھ رہے اور اپنے شاگر دیا نوکر کے ہاتھ جانور بھیج دے اور اگر موجز بیمار ہوکر بیٹھ رہا تب بھی جواب یہی ہے۔ مبسوط کی روایت پر اور امام کرختی نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ یہ بھی عذر ہے کیونکہ یہ بھی ضرر سے خالی نہیں۔ پس بوقت ضرورت اس سے یہ ضرر دور کیا جائے گا نہ کہ بحالت اختیار ۔
تشریح قوله ثم بداله الخ جمہرہ اور جمل وغیرہ میں ہے کہ بد (ن) بدوا ، بداء بمعنی ظاہر ہونا ہے اور بدالی فی هذا الامر اس وقت بولتے ہیں جب پہلی رائے بدل جائے ۔ اس سے کہتے ہیں فلان ذوبد وات کہ فلاں شخص مختلف را یوں والا ہے یعنی اس کی رائے میں کوئی پختگی نہیں ہے۔
-
ثم بداله من السفر کا مطلب یہ ہوا ۔ بداله صلاح خلاف ماقصده من السفر یعنی پہلے جو سفر کا ارادہ کیا تھا اس کے خلاف کی بہتری ظاہر ہوئی ۔ پس بدا فعل کا فاعل محذوف نہیں بلکہ بدلالت محل کلام ۔ فاعل مضمر ہے لہذا اس کی طرف ضمیر کا لونا صیح ہے كما في قولهم ان كان غدًا فائتنى ان كان مانحن فيه غدًا فائتنی عبارت کتاب کا مطلب اور مسئلہ کا حکم ترجمہ سے واضح ہے ۔
And whoever hires an animal to travel upon, then he changes his mind about the travel, it is an excuse, because if he proceeds according to the contract, excessive harm would befall him, because perhaps he was going for Hajj and its time passes, or to seek a debtor and he becomes present, or for trade and he becomes bankrupt. And if the hirer (mukari) changes his mind, it is not an excuse, because he can sit and send the animals with his student or his employee. And if the lessor (mu'ajir) falls ill and sits, then the answer is like this according to the riwaya of al-Asl, and al-Karkhi mentioned that it is an excuse, because it is not free from harm, so harm is repelled from him at the time of necessity, not at the time of choice.
Translation: Someone hired an animal to travel on it, then his opinion changed, so this is an excuse because if he acts according to the contract, excessive harm will befall him, because it is possible he was going for Hajj and its time passed, or he was going to seek a debtor but he became present, or he wanted to go for trade but he became bankrupt. And if the one who gives the animal on hire changes his opinion, it is not an excuse because it is possible for him to sit himself and send the animal with his student or servant. And if the lessor becomes ill and sits, even then the answer is the same according to the riwaya of al-Mabsut. And Imam al-Karkhi mentioned that this is also an excuse because it is not free from harm. So at the time of necessity this harm will be repelled, not at the time of choice.
Explanation of the statement 'then he changed his mind': In Jamhara and Jamal etc., it is said that 'bada' (with nun) bada', bada' means to become apparent, and 'bada lahu fi hadha al-amr' is said when the first opinion changes. From this they say 'fulan dhu badawat' meaning so-and-so has various opinions, i.e., there is no firmness in his opinion.
The meaning of 'thumma bada lahu min al-safar' is: 'bada lahu salah khi'af ma qasada min al-safar' i.e., the benefit contrary to what he intended from travel became apparent. So the subject of the verb 'bada' is not omitted, but by implication of the context of speech, the subject is implied, so the pronoun referring to it is correct, as in their saying 'in kana ghadan fa'tini' [if it is tomorrow, then come to me] meaning 'in kana ma nahnu fihi ghadan fa'tini'. The meaning of the book's expression and the ruling of the issue are clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on when a change of intention regarding travel after hiring an animal constitutes a valid excuse (udhr) to terminate the ijarah contract. The passage type is a ruling with rational proof. The author first states that if the hirer (musta'jir) changes his mind about traveling, it is an excuse because proceeding would cause him excessive harm: for example, he might have intended Hajj and the time passes, or he was going to seek a debtor who now appears, or he planned trade but becomes bankrupt. On the other hand, if the owner (mukari) changes his mind, it is not an excuse because he can still send the animal with a student or employee without harm. However, if the lessor (mu'ajir) himself falls ill and cannot travel, according to the riwaya of al-Asl (the original ruling) it is still not an excuse, but Imam al-Karkhi considers it an excuse because illness is not free from harm, and harm should be repelled in necessity but not in voluntary choice. The Urdu commentary then provides linguistic explanation of the term 'bada lahu' and clarifies the meaning of the phrase 'thumma bada lahu min al-safar' as a change to a better course contrary to the previous intention. This lesson highlights the Hanafi principle of preventing harm (darar) as the basis for allowing contract termination, but with strict conditions to prevent abuse.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legally recognized excuse that allows termination of a binding contract to prevent harm.
مكارى (mukari): The person who hires out an animal or vehicle; the lessor.
مواجر (mu'ajir): The person who gives something on ijarah; the lessor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hirer's change of intention considered a valid excuse (udhr) to terminate the ijarah, but the owner's change is not?
A) Because the hirer is more important than the owner.
B) Because proceeding with the contract would cause the hirer excessive harm but not the owner.
C) Because the owner can always send the animal with someone else.
D) Because the hirer made the first decision.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, only the hirer's change of intention that causes unavoidable harm is an udhr; the owner's change is not.

The Illness of the Lessor: A Contested Excuse in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on udhr due to illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi difference on whether illness of the lessor is an excuse in ijarah and evaluate the reasoning of Imam al-Karkhi versus the position of al-Asl.

Core Idea
Illness of the lessor is not an excuse according to al-Asl, but Imam al-Karkhi considers it an excuse because harm is not free.

The previous lesson covered the hirer's change of mind. Now we turn to a contested point: if the lessor (mu'ajir) falls ill, does that give him the right to cancel the ijarah? Two positions within the Hanafi school exist.

Guided Path
1
State the position of al-Asl
According to the riwaya of al-Asl (the main books), illness of the mu'ajir is not an udhr. The lessor can still send the animal with a representative.
This is analogous to the owner's change of mind not being an udhr.
2
State the position of al-Karkhi
Imam al-Karkhi holds that illness is an udhr because it is not free from harm. The lessor suffers inherently from illness.
He argues that harm should be repelled at the time of necessity.
3
Compare the evidence
Al-Asl relies on the analogy with the owner's voluntary change; Karkhi emphasizes the inevitable harm from illness, which is beyond the lessor's control.
The difference hinges on whether the harm is avoidable (voluntary) or unavoidable (illness).
4
Determine the fatwa
The text notes both positions, with the default being the riwaya of al-Asl (illness not an udhr). However, Karkhi's view is cited as an alternative, indicating it is not rejected outright.
In practice, the mufta bihi remains the position of al-Asl, but the ikhtilaf is acknowledged.

Selling a Hired Slave Does Not Terminate Ijarah
Clarifies that selling a hired slave is not an udhr for the lessor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that selling a hired slave is not a valid excuse to terminate the ijarah, and apply the Hanafi principle that only actual harm (darar) justifies contract termination.

Core Idea
Loss of potential profit from sale is not harm; contracts remain valid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the stability of contracts is paramount. This lesson examines a classic case: a lessor sells a hired slave but wants to end the lease. The text clarifies that mere loss of profit does not constitute harm (darar) sufficient to void the contract, protecting the lessee's rights and public interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act under consideration? The lessor sells the hired slave. Is this a valid excuse to terminate the ijarah? The ruling is 'no' (it is not an udhr).
This ruling is agreed upon in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is rational: continuing the contract causes no harm to the lessor; he only loses the immediate profit from the sale. Loss of profit is an extra matter ('amr za'id) and not actual harm.
Harm (darar) is a key principle in Hanafi contract law.
3
Recognize the broader principle
If loss of profit were accepted as an excuse, no ijarah would be secure, disrupting people's needs. This reflects the Hanafi concern for contract stability and public interest.
The ruling is derived from the principle that contracts are binding unless actual harm occurs.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the sale's validity
While selling is not an excuse, there is disagreement on whether the sale itself is valid or suspended. The preferred view (al-Sarakhsi, Sadr al-Shahid) holds that the sale is suspended until the lessee's right ends.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the main ruling that selling is not an udhr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن آجر عبده ثم باعه فليس بعذر لانه لا يلزمه الضرر بالمضى على موجب العقد وانما يفوته الاسترباح وانه امر زائد
ترجمہ کسی نے اپنا غلام اجارہ پر دیا پھر اس کو فروخت کر دیا تو یہ عذر نہیں ہے کیونکہ مقتضاء عقد کے موافق چلنے میں اس کو کوئی ضرر لازم نہیں آتا بلکہ بالفعل نفع اٹھانا فوت ہوتا ہے اور یہ ایک امر زائد ہے۔
تشریح قوله ومن آجر عبده .. الخ
ایک شخص نے اپنا غلام اجارہ پر دیا پھر اس کو فروخت کر دیا تو یہ عذر نہیں ہے بالا تفاق اس سے اجارہ میچ نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے کہ مقتضاء عقد کے موافق چلنے میں اس کو کوئی سر ر لاحق نہیں ہوتا بلکہ صرف بالفعل نفع اٹھانا فوت ہوتا ہے جو امر زائد ہے اگر اس کی وجہ سے بھی اجارہ کو توڑ دیا جائے تو پھر تو کوئی اجارہ بھی صحیح سالم نہیں رہ سکتا۔ پس لوگوں کی ضرورتیں معطل ہو کر رہ جائیں گی ۔
فائدہ پھر صورت مذکورہ میں موجز اپنے غلام کو فروخت کرنا جائز ہے یا نہیں؟ اس میں الفاظ روایات کا اختلاف ہے۔ شیخ شمس الائمہ سرخسی نے اصل یعنی مبسوط کے اجارات کی شرح میں کہا ہے کہ صحیح روایت یہ ہے کہ بیچ حق مستاجر ساقط ہونے تک موقوف رہے گی اور مستاجر اس بیع کو تو ڑ نہیں سکتا صدر شہید کا میلان اسی طرف ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ مفتی اس کے جواب میں یوں لکھے کہ مستاجر کے حق میں یہ بیع جائز نہیں۔
And whoever hires out his slave then sells him, it is not an excuse, because no harm befalls him by proceeding according to the contract, but rather he only loses profit, and that is an extra matter.
Translation: Someone hired out his slave then sold him, so this is not an excuse because acting according to the contract causes him no harm, but rather the actual profit is lost, and that is an extra matter.
Explanation of the statement 'And whoever hires out his slave...': A person hired out his slave then sold him, so this is not an excuse by agreement; the ijarah cannot be nullified by it because acting according to the contract causes him no harm, but only the actual profit is lost, which is an extra matter. If the ijarah were broken due to this, then no ijarah would remain sound, and people's needs would be disrupted.
Benefit: Then, in the mentioned case, is it permissible for the lessor to sell his slave or not? There is disagreement in the wordings of narrations. Shaykh Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said in the commentary of al-Asl (al-Mabsut) on ijarah that the correct narration is that the sale will be suspended until the right of the lessee (musta'jir) is dropped, and the lessee cannot cancel the sale. Sadr al-Shahid leans towards this, to the extent that the mufti writes in response that this sale is not permissible with respect to the lessee's right.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling that selling a hired slave does not constitute a valid excuse (udhr) to terminate the ijarah contract. The passage type is a ruling with rational proof. The reasoning is that by continuing the contract, the lessor (mu'ajir) suffers no harm (darar); he only loses the immediate profit (istirbah) from the sale, which is an extra benefit beyond the contract. If such loss of profit were accepted as an udhr, then no ijarah contract could be secure, disrupting people's needs. The commentary adds a benefit (fa'idah) discussing the permissibility of the sale: according to al-Sarakhsi and Sadr al-Shahid, the sale is suspended until the lessee's right to service is extinguished, and the lessee cannot void the sale. This ruling underscores the Hanafi principle that only actual harm—not mere loss of potential profit—justifies contract termination.

Key Terms
استرباح (istirbah): The act of taking profit or gaining profit from a transaction.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The person who hires the slave; the lessee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

When a Tailor's Insolvency or Change of Profession Excuses Termination of Ijarah
Examines when a tailor's insolvency or change of profession excuses ijarah termination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of udhr in ijarah to distinguish between valid and invalid excuses based on harm and availability of alternatives.

Core Idea
Insolvency of a tailor working for himself is an excuse; changing profession is not if the work can be delegated.

A tailor hires a boy for sewing. Later, the tailor becomes insolvent or decides to switch to money-changing. Can he terminate the ijarah? The Hanafi school answers based on whether he suffers unavoidable harm. This lesson breaks down three scenarios from al-Asl, examining the concepts of capital (ra's al-mal), harm (darar), and the possibility of delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Extract the three distinct cases: (1) tailor who works for himself becomes insolvent, (2) tailor who works for wages becomes insolvent, (3) tailor wants to change profession from sewing to money-changing. Note the ruling for each.
The third scenario is compared to hiring a shop.
2
Understand the rationale of harm (darar)
For each scenario, determine whether proceeding with the contract would cause harm to the tailor. The key is whether his objective (capital) is lost or if he can avoid harm by delegating work.
Harm is the central criterion for udhr in Hanafi ijarah.
3
Distinguish between tailor working for himself vs. for wages
A tailor working for himself (charging for finished product) uses cloth as capital; if bankrupt, he loses his capital. A wage-worker uses only tools (thread, needle), which are not capital; insolvency is not possible here.
This distinction is the ta'wil: the ruling applies only to the self-employed tailor.
4
Compare change of profession: boy vs. shop
If the tailor has a hired boy, he can delegate sewing to the boy while he does money-changing. Thus no harm. If he hired a shop specifically for sewing, he cannot do two jobs in different places alone; thus change is an excuse.
Key difference: one worker vs. two workers.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the ta'wil
Some later scholars argue that the ta'wil restricting udhr to tailors working for themselves is unnecessary; even wage-workers could be unable due to loss of reputation. The mufta bihi applies the general principle of harm.
This ikhtilaf shows that legal reasoning is not monolithic even within a school.

He said: And if a tailor hires a boy, then becomes insolvent and leaves the work, it is an excuse because harm befalls him by proceeding according to the contract due to the loss of his objective which is his capital. The interpretation of the issue is a tailor who works for himself; as for one who sews for wages, his capital is thread, needle, and scissors, so insolvency does not materialize for him. And if he intends to leave sewing and work in money-changing, it is not an excuse because he can make the boy sit for sewing in one corner while he works in money-changing in another corner. And this is contrary to the case when he hires a shop for sewing, then intends to leave it and engage in another work, where it is made an excuse, mentioned in al-Asl, because one person cannot combine two [works]. But here the workers are two persons, so each can do his work.

Commentary: A tailor appointed a boy on annual wages of forty rupees, then he became insolvent and left the work, so it is an excuse; the ijarah will be rescinded because proceeding according to the contract would cause harm. Because his objective was lost, i.e., his capital is thread and needle, in which insolvency cannot be verified. And it is also said that there is no need for interpretation because sometimes one who works for wages also becomes unable, such as when people become aware of his betrayal and he...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage provides three distinct scenarios regarding udhr in ijarah involving a tailor. The passage type is a ruling with rational proof and includes a contrast with hiring a shop. First, if a tailor (who works for himself) hires a boy for sewing and then becomes insolvent (iflas), it is an udhr because his capital (ra's al-mal) is at stake; proceeding would cause him harm. However, if the tailor works for wages (executing others' orders), his capital is only thread, needle, and scissors, so insolvency does not affect him. Second, if the tailor wants to change profession from sewing to money-changing (sarf), it is not an udhr because he can have the boy continue sewing in one corner while he does money-changing in another. This is contrasted with the case of hiring a shop for sewing: if he hires a shop specifically for sewing and then wants to change to another work, it is an udhr because one person cannot perform two jobs simultaneously in different places. Since here there are two workers (the tailor and the boy), they can work separately. The commentary adds that some scholars say no need for the interpretation restricting the tailor to one 'working for himself', because even a wage-worker could become unable due to loss of reputation. This lesson illustrates the Hanafi principle that udhr is based on actual harm and impossibility, and the availability of alternatives prevents udhr.

Key Terms
إفلاس (iflas): Insolvency; inability to pay debts or continue business due to loss of capital.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): Capital; the principal investment. For a tailor working for himself, it is the cloth etc.; for a wage-worker, it is tools.
خياط (khayyat): Tailor; in this context, either one who sews for himself (charging for the finished product) or one who sews for wages (executing orders with customer's cloth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a tailor's insolvency considered a valid excuse (udhr) to terminate the ijarah of a hired boy?
A) Only when the tailor works for himself and his capital is lost.
B) Only when the tailor works for wages and his tools are lost.
C) In all cases of insolvency.
D) Only when the tailor intends to change profession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, an excuse for termination is based on actual unavoidable harm; if the harm can be avoided by delegation or if the capital is unaffected, no excuse exists.

When Can You Cancel an Ijarah? The Hanafi Concept of Valid Excuse (Udhr)
Ruling on excuses for terminating ijarah with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings on excuses for terminating ijarah (selling hired slave vs tailor's insolvency) and identify the underlying principle of harm vs loss of profit.

Core Idea
An excuse to cancel ijarah requires real harm, not just loss of expected profit.

This passage from al-Asl addresses a common question: can you cancel a rental contract (ijarah) if circumstances change? The Hanafi school distinguishes between losing the hired item (which is not an excuse) and losing the ability to perform the work (which is an excuse). Understanding this distinction prevents wrongful termination and ensures both parties' rights are protected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and their rulings
Read the passage and list the two scenarios: (1) a hired slave is sold, and (2) a tailor becomes insolvent. Note the ruling for each: selling is not an excuse, insolvency is an excuse.
The passage explicitly states 'ليس بعذر' for selling, and implies approval for the tailor's insolvency as an excuse.
2
Determine the illa (cause) for each ruling
Analyze why one is an excuse and the other is not. The passage explains that with the slave, the hirer can still use the service (e.g., seat the boy for sewing in one corner while the tailor works elsewhere). With the tailor's insolvency, the work cannot proceed, causing direct harm. The illa for excuse is unavoidable harm to the contract, not mere loss of profit.
The contrast with hiring a shop for tailoring and then wanting to use it for another trade highlights that one person cannot be in two places at once—that is harm.
3
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given the principle, test a similar case: a hired cow is sold. Since the cow can still be used by the new owner, the hire continues. But if the hired worker becomes ill and cannot work, that is likely an excuse. Check the condition of unavoidable harm.
Remember: the key is whether the contract can still be performed without additional harm to the hirer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس بعذر لأنه يمكنه أن يقعد الغلام للخياطة في ناحية وهو يعمل في الصرف في ناحية وهذا بخلاف ما إذا استأجر دكانا للخياطة فأراد أن يتركها ويشتغل بعمل آخر حيث جعله عذرا ذكره في الأصل لأن الواحد لا بَينَ مِلْيَيْنِ [؟] أما هنا العامل شخصان فأمكنهما
It is not an excuse, because he can seat the boy for sewing in one corner and he works in moneychanging in another corner. And this is contrary to the case where he hires a shop for sewing and wants to leave it and engage in another work, where he made it an excuse, mentioned in al-Asl, because one cannot be in two places [؟]. But here the workers are two persons, so it is possible for them.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage discusses what constitutes a valid excuse (udhr) in ijarah contracts. The first ruling: if a person hires out his slave and then sells him, this is not a valid excuse to terminate the ijarah because the hirer experiences no direct harm—only the loss of potential profit, which is an additional matter. The second ruling: a tailor hires a boy, then becomes insolvent and stops working—this is an excuse because the tailor suffers harm as his capital (thread, needle, scissors) is lost with no return. However, if the tailor wants to switch from tailoring to moneychanging while keeping the boy, he cannot terminate the ijarah because he can have the boy sew in one corner while he does moneychanging in another; the two persons can work simultaneously. This is contrasted with the case where someone rents a shop for tailoring and then wants to use it for another trade—that is an excuse because one person cannot do two jobs in one place.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legally recognized excuse that allows termination of an ijarah contract without penalty.
مستأجر (musta’jir): The person who hires the slave's services.
خياط (khayyaṭ): Tailor; used in the example of hiring a boy for sewing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is selling a hired slave not a valid excuse to terminate the ijarah?
A) Selling is always forbidden during ijarah
B) The hirer can still obtain the service from the new owner
C) The hirer suffers no real harm, only loss of potential profit
D) The slave's service is not affected

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, an excuse to terminate requires actual harm to the contract, not merely a change in ownership or loss of potential profit.
